Advanced Java Application : SpringBoot Reactive  , GCP pub sub , AWS , Azure , Kafka
Behavioral Design Pattern: Observer
Realworld Example of Observer Design pattern
Limitation Of Observer Design Pattern



Behavioral Design pattern 
                                 - it descrive how classess and object will interact with each other.
	              Ex. Observer Design pattern





Observer Design pattern Example:

public interface Observer {
    public void notification(String handle, String tweet);

}

public interface Subject {
    public void addSubscriber(Observer observer);
    public void removeSubscriber(Observer observer);
    public void notifySubscribers(String tweet);

}



public class YTTechnicalExpert implements Subject {
	
    protected List<Observer> observers = new ArrayList<Observer>();
    protected String name;
    protected String handle;

    public YTTechnicalExpert(String name, String handle) {
        super();

        this.name = name;

        this.handle = "#" + handle;

    }

    public String getName() {
        return name;

    }

    public void setName(String name) {

        this.name = name;

    }

    public String getHandle() {

        return handle;

    }

    public void tweet(String tweet) {
        System.out.printf("\nName: %s, Tweet: %s\n", name, tweet);

        notifySubscribers(tweet);
    }

    public synchronized void addSubscriber(Observer observer) {

        observers.add(observer);
    }
    public synchronized void removeSubscriber(Observer observer) {
        observers.remove(observer);

    }

    public void notifySubscribers(String tweet) {

        observers.forEach(observer -> observer.notification(handle, tweet));

    }
}


public class Follower implements Observer {
    protected String name;
    public Follower(String name) {
        super();
        this.name = name;

    }
    public void notification(String handle, String tweet) {
        System.out.printf("'%s' received notification from Handle: '%s', Tweet: '%s'\n", name, handle, tweet);

    }
}

Creational Design Pattern: Simple factory

#Simple factory:
	- Multiple types can be implemented and the choice is based on criteria.
	- we simply put the instantiation logic to a seperate class and most commonly 
	to a static method of a class.
	

#Simple factory using Java Class:


	 Post

	      BlogPost
	      NewsPost
	      ProductPost


public abstract class Post {
	private long id;
	private String title;
	private String content;
	//
}

public class NewsPost extends Post{

	private String newsHeadLine;
	//
}

class  BlogPost  extends Post{
	private String author;
	//
}
class  ProductPost extends Post{
	private String prodName;
	//

}


public class PostFactory {
	
	public static Post createPost(String type) {
		switch(type) {
		case "blog":
			return new BlogPost();
		case "news":
			return new NewsPost();
		case "product":
			return new ProductPost();
		default:
			throw new IllegalArgumentException("post type is unknown");
		}
		
	}
}

public class Client {

	public static void main(String[] args) {
		Post post = PostFactory.createPost("blog");
		System.out.println(post);
		
		Post post1 = PostFactory.createPost("news");
		System.out.println(post1);
	}
}

#Example Of Simple factory from JAVA API

java.text.NumberFormat



#Limitation of Simple Factory:
	- the criteria used by simple factory to decide which object to instantiate  can get more complex overtime.
                  - if you find in such situation the use factory method.



# Simple Factory    and Factory Method
 Simple Factory
- simply we move our instanciation logic from client code
Factory Method
- it is more useful  when you want to deligate object creation in the sub class.
---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Advanced Java Application : 
Creational Design Pattern: factory method
Example from Java API
Limitation Of  factory method


#factory method
 - we want to move object creation logic from our code to a seperate class.
- we use this pattern when we do not know in advance which class we may need 
   to instanciate
- we let sub class decide which object to instanciate by overriding a factory method. 


#Java class Implemenattion

public abstract class Message {
	public abstract String getContent();
}

public class TextMessage extends Message {
@Override
	public String getContent() {
		return "Test Message";
	}
}

public class JSONMessage extends Message {
	@Override
	public String getContent(){
		return "JSONMessage";
	}
}


public abstract class MessageCreator {
	public  Message    getContent() {
		Message  msg= createMessage();
		return msg;
	}
	//factory
	public abstract Message createMessage();
}

public class TextMessageCreator extends MessageCreator{
	@Override
	public Message createMessage() {
		// TODO Auto-generated method stub
		return new TextMessage();
	}
}

public class JSONMessageCreator  extends MessageCreator{
	@Override
	public Message createMessage() {
		// TODO Auto-generated method stub
		return new JSONMessage();
	}
}


public class Client {
	public static void main(String[] args) {
		printMessage(new JSONMessageCreator());
		printMessage(new TextMessageCreator());
	}
	public static void printMessage(MessageCreator  creator) {
	
		Message  msg= creator.getContent();
		System.out.println(msg);
	}
}

#Factory method from java  api

java.util.Collection / java.util.AbstractCollection


Limitation :
- more complex to implement becoz more classess are involved.

#Simple factory         and      Factory   Method    and  Abstract Factory 
 
Simple factory  
- just one implementation with no sub classing

 Factory   Method
- you have some generic processing (sub classing)

 Abstract Factory 
- we create family of object of same kind

# Adapter
- using  adapter two incompatible interface will communicate with each other.
- In adapter we have an existing object which provide the functionality that client need.
   but the client code can not use the object because it expect the object with different interface.
- we make the existing object work with client by adpting the object to client expected interface.

				Example from Java API
				Limitation Of  Adapter

#Implemenation Of Adapter Design Pattern:

public interface MediaPlayer{
	   void playMusic(String audioType, String fileName);
}


public class AudioPlayer implements MediaPlayer {
    private MediaAdapter mediaAdapter;
    @Override
    public void playMusic(String audioType, String fileName) {
        //the mp3 format is supported by AudioPlayer itself and it doesn't need adapter here.
        if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase("mp3")) {
            System.out.println("Playing mp3 file: " + fileName);
        }
        //to support other formats, we will need the MediaAdapter
        else if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase("vlc") || audioType.equalsIgnoreCase("mp4")) {
            mediaAdapter = new MediaAdapter(audioType);
            mediaAdapter.playMusic(audioType, fileName);
        } else {
            System.out.println("The given format: " + audioType + " is not supported");
        }
    }
}


public interface AdvancedMediaPlayer {
	   void playVlcPlayer(String fileName);
	   void playMp4Player(String fileName);
}



public class Mp4MusicPlayer implements AdvancedMediaPlayer {

    @Override
    public void playVlcPlayer(String fileName) {
        //do nothing
    }

    @Override
    public void playMp4Player(String fileName) {
        System.out.println("Playing mp4 file: " + fileName);
    }
}


public class VlcMusicPlayer implements AdvancedMediaPlayer {
    @Override
    public void playVlcPlayer(String fileName) {
        System.out.println("Playing vlc file: " + fileName);
    }

    @Override
    public void playMp4Player(String fileName) {
        //do nothing
    }
}


public class MediaAdapter implements MediaPlayer {

    public static final String VLC = "vlc";
    public static final String MP_4 = "mp4";

    private AdvancedMediaPlayer advancedMusicPlayer;
    public MediaAdapter(String audioType) {
        if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase(VLC)) {
            advancedMusicPlayer = new VlcMusicPlayer();
        } else if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase(MP_4)) {
            advancedMusicPlayer = new Mp4MusicPlayer();
        }
    }

    @Override
    public void playMusic(String audioType, String fileName) {
        if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase(VLC)) {
            advancedMusicPlayer.playVlcPlayer(fileName);
        } else if (audioType.equalsIgnoreCase(MP_4)) {
            advancedMusicPlayer.playMp4Player(fileName);
        }
    }
}

#when to use  adapter design pattern ?

- when we want an object to use an existing incompatible interface.
- when we want to craete a middle layer for ceating a connection 
  between your modern code and legacy code.

#Limitation Of Adapter pattern:
- simple interface translation can result in the adapter showing different behavior.

Advanced Java Application : 
Structural Design Pattern: facade
Example from Java API
Limitation Of  facade

#facade
-A facade encapsulates a complex subsystem behind a simple interface. 
-It hides much of the complexity and makes the subsystem easy to use.


#Facade
-Client has to interact with large number of interface and classes in a sub system to get the result.
-So client get tightly coupled with those interface and classes.
Facade solve those problems.	
Facade provide a simple and unified interface  to a sub system. client will interact with the façade  now to get the same result.



#Java code implementation 

public class CarEngineFacade {
    private static int DEFAULT_COOLING_TEMP = 90;
    private static int MAX_ALLOWED_TEMP = 50;
    private FuelInjector fuelInjector = new FuelInjector();
    private AirFlowController airFlowController = new AirFlowController();
    private Starter starter = new Starter();
    private CoolingController coolingController = new CoolingController();
    private CatalyticConverter catalyticConverter = new CatalyticConverter();

    public void startEngine() {
        fuelInjector.on();
        airFlowController.takeAir();
        fuelInjector.on();
        fuelInjector.inject();
        starter.start();
        coolingController.setTemperatureUpperLimit(DEFAULT_COOLING_TEMP);
        coolingController.run();
        catalyticConverter.on();
    }

    public void stopEngine() {
        fuelInjector.off();
        catalyticConverter.off();
        coolingController.cool(MAX_ALLOWED_TEMP);
        coolingController.stop();
        airFlowController.off();
    }
}


//call only two function
	startEngine()
	stopEngine()

#Limitation of facade
It can be overused and can hide improperly designed API.

Advanced Java Application :  

		ArrayList<Employee>   arr = new ArrayList<>();
		arr.add(new  Employee(1001,"Srini","dev"));
		arr.add(new  Employee(1002,"Navin","dev"));
		arr.add(new  Employee(1003,"Nikhil","CTO"));
		
		System.out.println(arr);
		
		Iterator<Employee>   it = arr.iterator();
		while(it.hasNext()) {
			System.out.println(it.next());
		}


#Iterator
- Iterator  allows a way to access elements of an aggregate object in sequence while hiding the actual internal datastructure.
-Iterator object remember its position while iterating.


#Iterator using java class

//Iterator.java
public interface Iterator {
   public boolean hasNext();
   public Object next();
}

//Container.java
public interface Container {
   public Iterator getIterator();
}


public class NameRepository implements Container {

   public String names[] = {"Robert" , "John" ,"Julie" , "Lora"};

   @Override
   public Iterator getIterator() {
      return new NameIterator();
   }

   private class NameIterator implements Iterator {
      int index;
      @Override
      public boolean hasNext() {
         if(index < names.length){
            return true;
         }
         return false;
      }
      @Override
      public Object next() {
         if(this.hasNext()){
            return names[index++];
         }
         return null;
      }		
   }
}


public class IteratorPatternDemo {	
   public static void main(String[] args) {

      NameRepository namesRepository = new NameRepository();

      for(Iterator iter = namesRepository.getIterator(); iter.hasNext();){
         String name = (String)iter.next();
         System.out.println("Name : " + name);
      } 	
   }
}






Sprint1 - Data Modelling/DAO typically refers to the first sprint in a software development project where the focus is on designing the data model and implementing the DAO (Data Access Object) layer. Here's a breakdown of what each part means:


Data Modelling
This is the process of defining how data is structured and related in your application. It involves:

Identifying entities (e.g., User, Product, Order)
Defining attributes for each entity (e.g., User has name, email)
Establishing relationships (e.g., One User can have many Orders)
Creating ER diagrams or schema definitions
Mapping to database tables (for relational databases) or collections (for NoSQL)
This step ensures that the application has a solid foundation for storing and retrieving data efficiently.

DAO (Data Access Object) Layer
The DAO pattern is a design pattern used to abstract and encapsulate all interactions with the data source (like a database). It provides a clean separation between business logic and data access logic.


Key Features of DAO:
Encapsulation: All database operations are centralized in DAO classes.
CRUD Operations: DAO typically provides methods like create(), read(), update(), and delete() 
Abstraction: The rest of the application doesn't need to know how data is stored or retrieved.
Maintainability: Changes to the database or queries only affect the DAO layer.
Testability: Easier to mock DAO methods for unit testing.

Common DAO Components:
DAO Interface: Defines the standard methods for data access.
DAO Implementation: Contains the actual logic to interact with the database.
Entity/Model Classes: Represent the data structure.
Data Source: Manages database connections.
Transfer Objects (DTOs): Used to carry data between layers.


Sprint1 Context
In Sprint1, the team usually
Finalizes the data model based on business requirements.
Sets up the database schema.
Implements DAO interfaces and classes for core entities.
Ensures basic CRUD operations are working.
Prepares the foundation for service and controller layers in future

What is UML?
Purpose of UML in Software Development
Benefits of Using UML
Common UML Diagrams and Their Uses
Comparison : Structure vs Behavior vs Interaction Diagrams
How to Employ UML in Agile – Best Practices




What is UML?
UML (Unified Modeling Language) is a standardized visual modeling language used to describe, design, and document software systems. 
It provides a set of diagrams and notations to represent the structure, behavior, and interactions of system components.


Purpose of UML in Software Development

Purpose				Explanation
1. Visualize System Design		UML helps teams visualize the architecture and components of a system before coding starts.

2. Standardize Communication	UML diagrams provide a common language for developers, testers, business analysts, 
                                			and stakeholders.

3. Analyze Requirements	        	It enables clear modeling of functional and non-functional requirements through 
                                 			use case diagrams.

4. Design Software Components	UML supports object-oriented design, helping in creating maintainable and reusable systems.

5. Guide Implementation	        	UML diagrams act as a blueprint that guides developers during implementation.

6. Support Documentation		UML diagrams are excellent for creating detailed technical documentation for current
                                			and future teams.

7. Enable Better Testing and Maintenance	Helps QA and maintenance teams understand how modules interact,
                                               			 what to test, and where bugs may occur.













Benefits of Using UML

Improved Communication  -	Makes it easy for both technical and non-technical team members to understand 
                                		system behavior and design.

Better Planning	        -       Early visualization helps identify design flaws, dependencies, or missing requirements.

Faster Development      -	Well-defined models reduce ambiguity and make coding more efficient.

Reduced Risk            -	Early modeling highlights integration issues and logic gaps before implementation.

Facilitates Reusability	-       Object-oriented modeling promotes reusable design patterns and modules.

Easier Maintenance      -	A documented system is easier to understand, debug, and update.





Comparison : Structure vs Behavior vs Interaction Diagrams

UML diagrams are broadly categorized into three types:

Category	 	Purpose
Structure Diagrams	Show the static structure of a system – its components and their relationships.
                        		Ex.Class Diagram -Models classes and relationships
                                             	components-Class, attribute, method, association

Behavior Diagrams	Show the dynamic behavior of the system – how the system changes over time.
                        		Ex. Use Case Diagram -Shows system functionality from user’s perspective
                                             	components -Actor, use case, association


Interaction Diagrams	A subset of behavior diagrams that focus on object interactions – 
                        		how entities communicate and collaborate in processes.
                        		Ex.Interaction Overview -Diagram Combines sequence & activity diagram elements
                     	                      components-Interaction, activity, decision



Common UML Diagrams and Their Uses
Diagram Type			Purpose
Use Case Diagram	       		 Shows user interactions with the system – helps gather requirements.
Class Diagram			Describes the structure of the system with classes, attributes, methods, and relationships.
Sequence Diagram	        		Models the flow of messages between objects in a specific interaction.
Activity Diagram	       		 Shows workflow or processes – useful for business logic and operations.
State Diagram	              		  Represents state transitions of objects – useful for event-driven systems.
Component Diagram	        	Shows the physical components and how they interact – useful in large applications.
Deployment Diagram	        	Represents the deployment of components on hardware nodes – used in architecture design.






Why Use UML in Agile?

Agile Principle			UML’s Role

Face-to-face conversation	   UML provides a visual aid during team discussions.
Customer collaboration	        Use case diagrams help capture requirements with stakeholders.
Responding to change	        Models can be updated quickly with minimal effort.
Working software	                           UML helps design just enough architecture for rapid development.




How to Employ UML in Agile – Best Practices

UML Diagrams for Agile – By Stage
Agile Stage	                                			UML Diagram	         	Purpose

Requirement Gathering (Backlog Grooming)		Use Case Diagram	 	Capture user goals and system 
										interactions clearly.
Sprint Planning	                                			Activity Diagram	 	Model workflows and user stories 
										step-by-step.
Design						Class Diagram, Component Diagram   Define structure and responsibilities
										   of system modules.
Development (During Sprint)	                		Sequence Diagram, 
						State Machine Diagram	         Design interactions and object 
										state behavior.
Testing & QA					Activity Diagram, Sequence Diagram	Visualize test scenarios,
                                                                                						 business rules, and flows.
Deployment & Architecture	                		Deployment Diagram	         Model cloud/on-premise deployment setup.

Understanding and applying the SOLID principles and 
the DRY (Don't Repeat Yourself) principle significantly improves Java code maintainability, scalability, and reusability. 



What is SOLID?
SOLID is an acronym for five design principles in object-oriented programming:

S – Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)

O – Open/Closed Principle (OCP)

L – Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)

I – Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)

D – Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)

DRY - Don't Repeat Yourself















1. Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)
A class should have only one reason to change.

##Bad Example:
class UserService {
    public void registerUser(String email) {
        // logic to register user
    }

    public void sendWelcomeEmail(String email) {
        // logic to send email
    }
}


##Good Example (SRP Applied):

class UserService {
    public void registerUser(String email) {
        // logic to register user
    }
}
class EmailService {
    public void sendWelcomeEmail(String email) {
        // logic to send email
    }
}
Now UserService is responsible only for user registration, and EmailService handles emails.







2. Open/Closed Principle (OCP)
Software entities should be open for extension, but closed for modification.


##Bad Example:
class PaymentProcessor {
    public void process(String type) {
        if (type.equals("creditcard")) {
            // credit card logic
        } else if (type.equals("paypal")) {
            // PayPal logic
        }
    }
}

##Good Example (OCP Applied):

interface Payment {
    void pay();
}

class CreditCardPayment implements Payment {
    public void pay() {
        System.out.println("Paid with credit card");
    }
}

class PayPalPayment implements Payment {
    public void pay() {
        System.out.println("Paid with PayPal");
    }
}

class PaymentProcessor {
    public void process(Payment payment) {
        payment.pay();
    }
}
You can add new payment types without changing PaymentProcessor.









3. Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)
Subtypes must be substitutable for their base types.

- Child classes should never break the parent class' type definitions. 
According to this principle, a subclass should override the parent class's methods in a way that does
 not break functionality from a client's point of view




Bad Example:
class Bird {
    public void fly() {
        System.out.println("Flying");
    }
}

class Ostrich extends Bird {
    public void fly() {
        throw new UnsupportedOperationException("Ostriches can't fly");
    }
}



#Good Example (LSP Applied):

interface Bird {
    void eat();
}

interface FlyingBird extends Bird {
    void fly();
}

class Parrot implements FlyingBird {
    public void fly() { System.out.println("Parrot flying"); }
    public void eat() { System.out.println("Parrot eating"); }
}

class Ostrich implements Bird {
    public void eat() { System.out.println("Ostrich eating"); }
}













4. Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)
Clients should not be forced to depend on interfaces they do not use.

Bad Example:
interface Worker {
    void work();
    void eat();
}

class Robot implements Worker {
    public void work() { }
    public void eat() { } // not applicable for robot
}



Good Example (ISP Applied):
interface Workable {
    void work();
}

interface Eatable {
    void eat();
}

class Human implements Workable, Eatable {
    public void work() { }
    public void eat() { }
}

class Robot implements Workable {
    public void work() { }
}
























5. Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)
High-level modules should not depend on low-level modules. Both should depend on abstractions.




Bad Example:
class MySQLDatabase {
    public void connect() {
        System.out.println("Connected to MySQL");
    }
}

class oRACLEDatabase {
    public void connect() {
        System.out.println("Connected to oRACLE");
    }
}
class db2Database {
    public void connect() {
        System.out.println("Connected to MySQL");
    }
}



class Application {
    MySQLDatabase db = new MySQLDatabase();
    public void start() {
        db.connect();
    }
}

Good Example (DIP Applied):

interface Database {
    void connect();
}

class MySQLDatabase implements Database {
    public void connect() {
        System.out.println("Connected to MySQL");
    }
}


class Application {
    private Database db;

    public Application(Database db) {
        this.db = db;
    }

    public void start() {
        db.connect();
    }
}
Now we can inject any Database implementation, such as PostgresDatabase, into the application.







DRY Principle – Don't Repeat Yourself
Avoid duplicating logic or data. Reuse code with methods, classes, and inheritance/composition.

#Bad Example (Duplicate Code):

class Invoice {
    public double calculateTotal(double price, int qty) {
        return price * qty + price * qty * 0.18;
    }

    public double calculateDiscount(double price, int qty) {
        double total = price * qty + price * qty * 0.18;
        return total - 100;
    }
}

#Good Example (DRY Applied):

class Invoice {
    public double calculateTotal(double price, int qty) {
        return getPriceWithGST(price, qty);
    }

    public double calculateDiscount(double price, int qty) {
        return getPriceWithGST(price, qty) - 100;
    }

    private double getPriceWithGST(double price, int qty) {
        return price * qty * 1.18;
    }
}
Now we avoid repetition and improve maintainability.


Principle	Focus						Benefit
SRP		One responsibility per class			Easier to test and modify
OCP		Extend without modifying existing code		Flexible design
LSP		Subclass should be replaceable			Avoids unexpected behavior
ISP		Keep interfaces lean				Fewer changes ripple through code
DIP		Depend on abstractions				Easier to scale and test
DRY		Avoid duplicate code				Reduces bugs and effort

#Traditional Approach

1. create class
 class Employee{
     //

    // Employee getUserById(){

     }

 }
2.create testcase
 Class EmployeeTest{

     Employee getUserByIdTest(){

     }

 }

#TDD

1.create testcase  so that the function fail the test

2.create  class 

3.refractor the class so that the function pass the test

































//What is Test Driven Development (TDD)?
Test Driven Development (TDD) is a software development practice that focuses on creating 
unit test cases before developing the actual code. 

The TDD approach originates from the Agile manifesto principles and Extreme programming.
As the name suggests, the test process drives software development.

Moreover, it’s a structuring practice that enables developers and testers to obtain 
optimized code that proves resilient in the long term.

In TDD, developers create small test cases for every feature based on their initial understanding.


//Test Driven Development (TDD) Examples
    Calculator Function: When building a calculator function, a TDD approach would involve writing a test case for the “add” function and then writing the code for the process to pass that test. Once the “add” function is working correctly, additional test cases would be written for other functions such as “subtract”,  “multiply” and “divide”.
 
   User Authentication: When building a user authentication system, a TDD approach would involve writing a test case for the user login functionality and then writing the code for the login process to pass that test.Once the login functionality works correctly, additional test cases will be written for registration,  password reset, and account verification.
  
  E-commerce Website: When building an e-commerce website, a TDD approach would involve writing test cases for various features such as product listings, shopping cart functionality, and checkout process. 
     Tests would be written to ensure the system works correctly at each process stage, from adding items to the cart to completing the purchase.







#TDD Phases:How to perform TDD Test
Following steps define how to perform TDD test,

    Add a test.
    Run all tests and see if any new test fails.
    Write some code.
    Run tests and Refactor code.
    Repeat.

 


























#Benefits of Test Driven Development (TDD)

    -Fosters the creation of optimized code.
    -It helps developers better analyze and understand client requirements and request 
		clarity when not adequately defined.
    -Adding and testing new functionalities become much easier in the latter stages of development.
    -Test coverage under TDD is much higher compared to conventional development models.
		 The TDD focuses on creating tests for each functionality right from the beginning.
    -It enhances the productivity of the developer and leads to the development of a codebase that is
		 flexible and easy to maintain.






#Frameworks for Test Driven Development

	Based on unique programming languages, multiple frameworks support test driven development. 
	Listed below are a few popular ones.
    	-csUnit and NUnit are open source unit testing frameworks for .NET projects.
    	-PyUnit and DocTest: Popular Unit testing framework for Python.
    	-Junit: Widely used unit testing tool for Java
    	-TestNG: Another popular Java testing framework. This framework overcomes the limitations of Junit.
    	-Rspec: A testing framework for Ruby projects


#TDD:
The name itself suggest that first we need to write our test case,
then based on test case we have to provide actual business implementations.
 TEST
 DRIVEN
 DEVELOPEMENT


#why we need to write test case first ? 
- using TDD we can maintain 
   		A. code quality
    		B. code coverage
- TDD have its own life cycle

        
         RED    		 GREEN     		  REFACTOR
         failure test case     pass test case           provide the implementation


RED  - what is the possible solution to break my code.
GREEN - write enough code to pass the test case.
REFACTOR - inprove the code without changing its behavior

TDD, JUnit, and Mockito — along with simple Java code examples — to help you understand how to test effectively using Test-Driven Development.


What is Mockito?

Mockito is a mocking framework for Java that allows you to simulate (mock) dependencies in unit tests.
Useful for isolating components in tests
Avoids hitting actual database, web service, or third-party APIs



Setup:
1.Junit 5 dependency + Mockito dependency 

Example: JUnit + Mockito

We have a UserService that fetches user data from a UserRepository.
We’ll mock the repository so our tests don’t depend on a real database.

1. Maven Dependencies
Add these to your pom.xml:

<dependencies>
    <!-- JUnit 5 -->
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.junit.jupiter</groupId>
        <artifactId>junit-jupiter</artifactId>
        <version>5.10.2</version>
        <scope>test</scope>
    </dependency>

    <!-- Mockito -->
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.mockito</groupId>
        <artifactId>mockito-core</artifactId>
        <version>5.11.0</version>
        <scope>test</scope>
    </dependency>

    <!-- Mockito JUnit Integration -->
    <dependency>
        <groupId>org.mockito</groupId>
        <artifactId>mockito-junit-jupiter</artifactId>
        <version>5.11.0</version>
        <scope>test</scope>
    </dependency>
</dependencies>



2. Code to Test

User.java
public class User {
    private int id;
    private String name;

    public User(int id, String name) {
        this.id = id;
        this.name = name;
    }

    public int getId() { return id; }
    public String getName() { return name; }
}


UserRepository.java

public interface UserRepository {
    User findById(int id);
}


UserService.java
public class UserService {

    private final UserRepository userRepository;

    public UserService(UserRepository userRepository) {
        this.userRepository = userRepository;
    }

    public String getUserName(int id) {
        User user = userRepository.findById(id);
        if (user != null) {
            return user.getName();
        }
        return "Unknown User";
    }
}


3. Unit Test with JUnit + Mockito

UserServiceTest.java
import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
import org.junit.jupiter.api.extension.ExtendWith;
import org.mockito.InjectMocks;
import org.mockito.Mock;
import org.mockito.junit.jupiter.MockitoExtension;

import static org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions.assertEquals;
import static org.mockito.Mockito.*;


@ExtendWith(MockitoExtension.class) // Enables Mockito for JUnit 5
public class UserServiceTest {

    @Mock
    private UserRepository userRepository; // Mocked dependency

    @InjectMocks
    private UserService userService; // Class under test

    @Test
    void testGetUserName_UserExists() {
        // Arrange
        User mockUser = new User(1, "Alice");
        when(userRepository.findById(1)).thenReturn(mockUser);

        // Act
        String result = userService.getUserName(1);

        // Assert
        assertEquals("Alice", result);
        verify(userRepository, times(1)).findById(1); // Ensure method was called
    }

    @Test
    void testGetUserName_UserNotFound() {
        // Arrange
        when(userRepository.findById(2)).thenReturn(null);

        // Act
        String result = userService.getUserName(2);

        // Assert
        assertEquals("Unknown User", result);
        verify(userRepository, times(1)).findById(2);
    }
}


How It Works
@Mock creates a fake UserRepository.
@InjectMocks injects the mock into UserService.
when(...).thenReturn(...) defines mock behavior.
verify(...) checks if the method was called as expected.
No real DB calls happen — tests are isolated.

Oracle PLSQL:

PL/SQL (Procedural Language/Structured Query Language) is Oracle Corporation's procedural extension for SQL and the Oracle relational database. It enables users to combine SQL statements with procedural constructs, providing capabilities such as variables, loops, conditional statements, and exception handling. 


Data Types:

Scalar Data Types:
INTEGER: Stores whole numbers within a specified range.
NUMBER: Represents numeric values with optional precision and scale.
VARCHAR2 / CHAR: Used for storing character strings of variable or fixed length, respectively.
BOOLEAN: Holds Boolean values (TRUE, FALSE, or NULL).
DATE / TIMESTAMP: Stores date and time values with optional time zone information.
BINARY_INTEGER: Similar to INTEGER but limited to smaller values.


SET SERVEROUTPUT ON;
DECLARE
  f_name  CHAR(15 CHAR);
  l_name   VARCHAR2(15 CHAR);
BEGIN
  f_name := 'Srini  ';
  l_name  := 'Navin  ';
 
  DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('*' || f_name || '*');
  DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('*' || l_name || '*');
END;
/



Char					VarChar/VarChar2
It is Static Datatype(i.e Fixed Length)	It is Dynamic Datatype(i.e Variable Length)
It can lead to memory wastage		It manages Memory efficiently

The SET SERVEROUTPUT command specifies whether output from the DBMS_OUTPUT message buffer is redirected to standard output



DECLARE
  n1 PLS_INTEGER := 2147483647;
  n2 INTEGER := 1;
  s NUMBER;
BEGIN
  s := n1 + n2;
DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('The result is: ' || TO_CHAR(s));
END;
/



Composite Data Types:
%TYPE: This pseudo-type is used to declare variables that have the same data type as a specified database column or variable.
%ROWTYPE: Represents a complete row of data in a table or cursor result set.




#control flow statements

Selection Statement - if else if

Iterative Statement - for , while




PL/SQL (Procedural Language/Structured Query Language) provides various control structures that allow developers to control the flow of execution within their code. 

IF-THEN-ELSE Statement:
The IF-THEN-ELSE statement allows developers to execute a block of code conditionally based on a specified condition.
If the condition evaluates to true, the code inside the THEN block is executed; otherwise, the code inside the ELSE block is executed.


CASE Statement:
The CASE statement evaluates a set of conditions and executes the corresponding code block based on the first condition that evaluates to true.
It is similar to a switch-case statement in other programming languages.


LOOP Statements:
PL/SQL provides various loop statements such as LOOP, WHILE, and FOR loops to iterate over a block of code until a certain condition is met.
The LOOP statement creates an infinite loop that continues until explicitly terminated using an EXIT statement.
The WHILE loop executes a block of code repeatedly as long as a specified condition remains true.
The FOR loop iterates over a range of values or a collection and executes the loop body for each iteration.


DECLARE
    v_marks NUMBER := 78;
    v_grade CHAR(1);
BEGIN
    -- IF-THEN-ELSE example
    IF v_marks >= 90 THEN
        v_grade := 'A';
    ELSIF v_marks >= 75 THEN
        v_grade := 'B';
    ELSIF v_marks >= 60 THEN
        v_grade := 'C';
    ELSE
        v_grade := 'F';
    END IF;

    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Grade using IF: ' || v_grade);

    -- CASE example
    CASE
        WHEN v_grade = 'A' THEN
            DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Excellent Performance!');
        WHEN v_grade = 'B' THEN
            DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Good Job!');
        WHEN v_grade = 'C' THEN
            DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('You Passed.');
        ELSE
            DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Better luck next time.');
    END CASE;

    -- LOOP example
    FOR i IN 1..3 LOOP
        DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Attempt ' || i);
    END LOOP;
END;
/




DECLARE
    counter NUMBER := 1;
BEGIN
    WHILE counter <= 5 LOOP
        DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Counter value: ' || counter);
        counter := counter + 1; -- increment to avoid infinite loop
    END LOOP;
END;
/


#plsql string function


    PLSQL  Function :
         - modularity
         - reusability

          		- Pre-Defined function	
				TRIM(v_text)
				UPPER(v_text)
				LOWER(v_text)
				INITCAP(v_text)
				

	 		- User Defined function


				return            input
				function    method(x,y){

					return  x+y

				}
                                              operation
	


-- Create a function to calculate bonus
CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION get_bonus (

                      p_salary NUMBER

  ) RETURN NUMBER
IS
    v_bonus NUMBER;

BEGIN

    v_bonus := p_salary * 0.10;
    RETURN v_bonus;

END;
/

























DECLARE
    v_text VARCHAR2(50) := '  Hello World  ';
BEGIN
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Original text: "' || v_text || '"');

    -- TRIM: remove spaces from both ends
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Trimmed: "' || TRIM(v_text) || '"');

    -- UPPER: convert to uppercase
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Uppercase: ' || UPPER(v_text));

    -- LOWER: convert to lowercase
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Lowercase: ' || LOWER(v_text));

    -- INITCAP: capitalize each word
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Initcap: ' || INITCAP(v_text));

    -- LENGTH: number of characters
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Length: ' || LENGTH(v_text));

    -- SUBSTR: extract part of string
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Substring (1-5): ' || SUBSTR(v_text, 1, 5));

    -- INSTR: find position of a substring
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Position of "World": ' || INSTR(v_text, 'World'));

    -- REPLACE: replace part of string
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Replace "World" with "Oracle": ' || REPLACE(v_text, 'World', 'Oracle'));
END;
/

Set<String> sh=new HashSet<>();  100000 - 30 sec
        sh.add("E");
        sh.add("C");
        sh.add("D");
        sh.add("B");
        sh.add("A");
        System.out.println(sh);

        Set<String> tsh=new TreeSet<>(); 100000 - 5 min
        tsh.add("E");
        tsh.add("C");
        tsh.add("D");
        tsh.add("B");
        tsh.add("A");
        System.out.println(tsh);

        Set<String> lsh=new LinkedHashSet<>();
        lsh.add("E");
        lsh.add("C");
        lsh.add("D");
        lsh.add("B");
        lsh.add("A");
        System.out.println(lsh);



			E   C   D   B   A
HashSet
    - faster insertion and Retrival performance.

LinkedHashSet
    - return object based on the insertion order.
	
TreeSet
    - return object based on the sorted order.

  Project Using Maven -  Code Quality :   PMD

  pmd setup



Code Quality tool: PMD

PMD is a static code analysis tool for Java (and a few other languages) that scans your source code without running it, looking for:

Bad practices (unused variables, empty catch blocks, duplicated code, etc.)
Possible bugs (null pointer risks, misused equals/hashCode, etc.)
Style violations (naming conventions, code complexity, etc.)
Maintainability issues (long methods, deeply nested code, etc.)


How PMD Works
Reads Java source code.
Applies pre-defined rules. 
Generates a report highlighting issues.
You fix the code based on these suggestions.

Maven Setup for PMD
Add PMD to your pom.xml:

<build>
    <plugins>
        <plugin>
            <groupId>org.apache.maven.plugins</groupId>
            <artifactId>maven-pmd-plugin</artifactId>
            <version>3.21.2</version>
            <configuration>
                <printFailingErrors>true</printFailingErrors>
                <failOnViolation>true</failOnViolation>
            </configuration>
            <executions>
                <execution>
                    <phase>verify</phase>
                    <goals>
                        <goal>check</goal>
                    </goals>
                </execution>
            </executions>
        </plugin>
    </plugins>
</build>


mvn pmd:check


public class ExamplePMD {
    public void processData() {
        int temp = 5; // Unused variable (PMD: UnusedLocalVariable)

        try {
            // Some risky code
        } catch (Exception e) {
            // Empty catch block (PMD: EmptyCatchBlock)
        }
    }
}
Expected PMD Output
If you run PMD on this code, you might get something like:

[INFO] PMD Failure: ExamplePMD.java:3  UnusedLocalVariable: Avoid unused local variables such as 'temp'.
[INFO] PMD Failure: ExamplePMD.java:6  EmptyCatchBlock: Avoid empty catch blocks; this can hide problems.



#Solution

public class ExamplePMD {
    public void processData() {
        int temp = calculateSomething();
        System.out.println("Value: " + temp);

        try {
            riskyOperation();
        } catch (Exception e) {
            System.err.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
        }
    }

    private int calculateSomething() {
        return 5;
    }

    private void riskyOperation() throws Exception {
        throw new Exception("Oops");
    }
}





==================================================================
Code Quality tool:  Check Style

Checkstyle is another static code analysis tool for Java 
-while PMD focuses on finding bugs and bad practices, 
-Checkstyle is mainly about enforcing coding standards and style guidelines.




What Checkstyle Does
Enforces naming conventions (classes, methods, variables).
Checks braces placement and indentation.
Ensures JavaDoc comments are present and formatted.
Limits line length and method length.
Prevents magic numbers.
Ensures code follows Google Style Guide or Sun Code Conventions (or your custom rules).


Maven Setup
Add to your pom.xml:

<build>
    <plugins>
        <plugin>
            <groupId>org.apache.maven.plugins</groupId>
            <artifactId>maven-checkstyle-plugin</artifactId>
            <version>3.3.1</version>
            <configuration>
                <configLocation>google_checks.xml</configLocation>
                <encoding>UTF-8</encoding>
                <consoleOutput>true</consoleOutput>
                <failsOnError>true</failsOnError>
            </configuration>
            <executions>
                <execution>
                    <phase>verify</phase>
                    <goals>
                        <goal>check</goal>
                    </goals>
                </execution>
            </executions>
        </plugin>
    </plugins>
</build>


/> mvn checkstyle:check


public class examplecheckstyle {  // Class name should start with uppercase

    public void process_data(){  // Method name should be lowerCamelCase without underscore
        int VALUE=10;  // Variable should be lowerCamelCase
        System.out.println( "Value: " + VALUE );  // Extra spaces, wrong formatting
    }
}

Possible Checkstyle Output

[ERROR] examplecheckstyle.java:1: Class name 'examplecheckstyle' must start with an uppercase letter. [TypeName]
[ERROR] examplecheckstyle.java:3: Method name 'process_data' must be lowerCamelCase. [MethodName]
[ERROR] examplecheckstyle.java:4: Variable 'VALUE' should be in lowerCamelCase. [LocalVariableName]
[ERROR] examplecheckstyle.java:5: 'System.out.println' has extra space after '(' [WhitespaceAfter]

#Solution
public class ExampleCheckstyle {

    public void processData() {
        int value = 10;
        System.out.println("Value: " + value);
    }
}




Checkstyle = focuses on formatting and naming conventions.
PMD = focuses on code smells and potential bugs.
They are often used together in CI pipelines to ensure code quality and consistency.

#Code Quality Tool : SonarQube

What is SonarQube?
SonarQube is a continuous inspection tool for code quality and security.
It analyzes source code for:
Bugs (code likely to cause errors)
Vulnerabilities (security risks)
Code smells (maintainability issues)
Test coverage (unit test percentage)
Duplications
Complexity (cyclomatic complexity)
Style violations


How It Works
Write Code → Commit to Git.
SonarQube Scanner runs (in CI or locally).
Code is analyzed using a set of rules.
A dashboard shows:
Bugs
Vulnerabilities
Code smells
Coverag
Maintainability rating (A–E)
Security rating (A–E)




Local Setup for Java
1. Install SonarQube
Download from: https://www.sonarsource.com/products/sonarqube/downloads/

Extract and run:
# Start SonarQube server
./bin/linux-x86-64/sonar.sh start
# or on Windows
bin\windows-x86-64\StartSonar.bat
Open browser: http://localhost:9000
Default login: admin / admin


2. Add Maven Plugin
In your Java project’s pom.xml:

<build>
    <plugins>
        <plugin>
            <groupId>org.sonarsource.scanner.maven</groupId>
            <artifactId>sonar-maven-plugin</artifactId>
            <version>3.10.0.2594</version>
        </plugin>
    </plugins>
</build>

3. Run Analysis

mvn clean verify sonar:sonar \
  -Dsonar.projectKey=MyJavaApp \
  -Dsonar.host.url=http://localhost:9000 \
  -Dsonar.login=your_generated_token
Java Example
Bad Code that SonarQube will flag:


public class ExampleSonar {

    public void process() {
        String name = null; // Possible NPE
        System.out.println(name.toLowerCase()); // Bug: NullPointerException risk
    }

    public void duplicateCodeExample() {
        System.out.println("Duplicate Code");
    }

    public void duplicateCodeExample2() {
        System.out.println("Duplicate Code"); // Sonar will flag as duplication
    }
}


What SonarQube Will Show
Bug: "Possible null pointer dereference in process()"
Code Smell: "Duplicated block of code found in 1 other location"
Maintainability Issue: "Consider refactoring to reduce duplication"


#Solution
public class ExampleSonar {

    public void process() {
        String name = getNameSafely();
        System.out.println(name.toLowerCase());
    }

    private String getNameSafely() {
        return "Default Name";
    }

    public void printMessage() {
        System.out.println("Duplicate Code");
    }
}


Why SonarQube is Powerful
Continuous monitoring — runs in CI/CD.
Custom rules — you can enforce your own standards.
Quality Gates — block merges if the code doesn’t meet requirements.
Trend tracking — see code quality over time.

#Code Quality Tool : JaCoCo





What is JaCoCo?
JaCoCo (Java Code Coverage) is a code coverage library for Java.
It measures how much of your code is executed when you run tests — helping you identify untested code.


It’s often used with:
JUnit (for unit tests)
Maven/Gradle (for builds)
SonarQube (to report coverage in dashboards)


What JaCoCo Measures
Line Coverage → % of executed lines.
Branch Coverage → % of decision branches tested (if/else).
Instruction Coverage → low-level JVM instructions executed.
Cyclomatic Complexity → number of possible execution paths.
Missed Classes/Methods → code never touched by tests.



Maven Setup
<build>
    <plugins>
        <plugin>
            <groupId>org.jacoco</groupId>
            <artifactId>jacoco-maven-plugin</artifactId>
            <version>0.8.11</version>
            <executions>
                <execution>
                    <goals>
                        <goal>prepare-agent</goal>
                    </goals>
                </execution>
                <execution>
                    <id>report</id>
                    <phase>verify</phase>
                    <goals>
                        <goal>report</goal>
                    </goals>
                </execution>
            </executions>
        </plugin>
    </plugins>
</build>


Example Java Code
public class Calculator {
    public int add(int a, int b) {
        return a + b;
    }
    public int subtract(int a, int b) {
        return a - b;
    }
    public int multiply(int a, int b) {
        return a * b;
    }
}

CalculatorTest.java (JUnit 5)
import static org.junit.jupiter.api.Assertions.assertEquals;
import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
public class CalculatorTest {

    @Test
    public void testAdd() {
        Calculator calc = new Calculator();
        assertEquals(5, calc.add(2, 3));
    }

    @Test
    public void testSubtract() {
        Calculator calc = new Calculator();
        assertEquals(1, calc.subtract(3, 2));
    }
}


Running JaCoCo
mvn clean test
mvn verify


JaCoCo will generate reports in:
target/site/jacoco/index.html
Open this HTML file in a browser — it shows:
Coverage per class/method/line
Highlighted lines covered (green) and missed (red)


Example Output
If we only test add() and subtract():
Line Coverage: ~66% (because multiply() was never tested)
Branch Coverage: N/A for this example (no if/else)
Missed method: multiply(int,int)


Solution:
@Test
public void testMultiply() {
    Calculator calc = new Calculator();
    assertEquals(6, calc.multiply(2, 3));
}

Code Quality With SpotBugs:

What is SpotBugs?
SpotBugs is a static code analysis tool for Java that scans compiled .class or .jar files 
(not just source code) to find potential bugs.

It’s the successor to FindBugs and can detect:
Possible null pointer dereferences
Infinite recursion / dead code
Misused equals/hashCode
Concurrency problems
Bad practice patterns


How It Works
Compile Java code → .class files.
SpotBugs runs pattern-matching algorithms on bytecode.
It reports bug patterns with descriptions, severity, and location.
Developers fix code before deploying.

Maven Setup
Add to your pom.xml:

<build>
    <plugins>
        <plugin>
            <groupId>com.github.spotbugs</groupId>
            <artifactId>spotbugs-maven-plugin</artifactId>
            <version>4.8.6.3</version>
            <configuration>
                <effort>Max</effort> <!-- More thorough analysis -->
                <threshold>Low</threshold> <!-- Show all issues -->
            </configuration>
            <executions>
                <execution>
                    <phase>verify</phase>
                    <goals>
                        <goal>check</goal>
                    </goals>
                </execution>
            </executions>
        </plugin>
    </plugins>
</build>



mvn spotbugs:check


Java Example with a Bug
public class ExampleSpotBugs {

    private String message;

    public ExampleSpotBugs(String message) {
        this.message = message;
    }

    public void printMessageLength() {
        // Potential NullPointerException — SpotBugs will catch this
        System.out.println("Length: " + message.length());
    }

    @Override
    public boolean equals(Object obj) {
        // Bug: Using '==' instead of .equals() for String comparison
        if (obj instanceof ExampleSpotBugs) {
            return ((ExampleSpotBugs) obj).message == this.message;
        }
        return false;
    }
}


What SpotBugs Will Flag
Example report:

[INFO] ExampleSpotBugs.java: Potential null pointer dereference of message in ExampleSpotBugs.printMessageLength()
[INFO] ExampleSpotBugs.java: String comparison using == in ExampleSpotBugs.equals(Object)


Solution:
public class ExampleSpotBugs {

    private String message;

    public ExampleSpotBugs(String message) {
        this.message = message;
    }

    public void printMessageLength() {
        if (message != null) {
            System.out.println("Length: " + message.length());
        } else {
            System.out.println("Message is null");
        }
    }

    @Override
    public boolean equals(Object obj) {
        if (obj instanceof ExampleSpotBugs) {
            return ((ExampleSpotBugs) obj).message != null &&
                   ((ExampleSpotBugs) obj).message.equals(this.message);
        }
        return false;
    }
}



Cursors:

Cursors are used in PL/SQL to retrieve and process multiple rows returned by a SQL query. 
PL/SQL supports both implicit and explicit cursors. 
Implicit cursors are automatically created for SQL statements like SELECT
while explicit cursors are declared explicitly using the CURSOR keyword.


CREATE TABLE employees_demo (
    emp_id     NUMBER,
    emp_name   VARCHAR2(50),
    salary     NUMBER
);

INSERT INTO employees_demo VALUES (1, 'John Doe', 50000);
INSERT INTO employees_demo VALUES (2, 'Jane Smith', 60000);
INSERT INTO employees_demo VALUES (3, 'Mike Brown', 55000);
COMMIT;


-- PL/SQL block with cursor
DECLARE
    -- 1. Declare the cursor
    CURSOR emp_cursor IS
        SELECT emp_id, emp_name, salary
        FROM employees_demo
        WHERE salary > 52000;

    -- Variables to hold fetched data
    v_id     employees_demo.emp_id%TYPE;
    v_name   employees_demo.emp_name%TYPE;
    v_salary employees_demo.salary%TYPE;
BEGIN
    -- 2. Open the cursor
    OPEN emp_cursor;

    LOOP
        -- 3. Fetch data into variables
        FETCH emp_cursor INTO v_id, v_name, v_salary;

        -- Exit loop when no more rows
        EXIT WHEN emp_cursor%NOTFOUND;

        -- 4. Process the fetched data
        DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('ID: ' || v_id || ', Name: ' || v_name || ', Salary: ' || v_salary);
    END LOOP;

    -- 5. Close the cursor
    CLOSE emp_cursor;
END;
/




Example: PL/SQL Function


-- Create a function to calculate bonus
CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION get_bonus (

       p_salary NUMBER

) RETURN NUMBER
IS
    v_bonus NUMBER;

BEGIN
    v_bonus := p_salary * 0.10;
    RETURN v_bonus;
END;
/


1. Call from PL/SQL block

DECLARE
    v_salary NUMBER := &enter_your_salary
    v_bonus  NUMBER;
BEGIN
    v_bonus := get_bonus(v_salary);
    DBMS_OUTPUT.PUT_LINE('Salary: ' || v_salary || ', Bonus: ' || v_bonus);
END;
/

        - Create a PLSQL block to call the function and print the addition result. 









Triggers:

Triggers are special types of stored procedures that are automatically executed in response to specific events occurring in the database. These events may include INSERT, UPDATE, or DELETE operations on tables. Triggers are useful for enforcing data integrity rules and implementing complex business logic.



Example: AFTER UPDATE Trigger

-- 1. Create a sample employee table
CREATE TABLE employees_demo (
    emp_id     NUMBER PRIMARY KEY,
    emp_name   VARCHAR2(50),
    salary     NUMBER
);

INSERT INTO employees_demo VALUES (1, 'John Doe', 50000);
INSERT INTO employees_demo VALUES (2, 'Jane Smith', 60000);
COMMIT;



-- 2. Create a table to store salary change logs
CREATE TABLE salary_audit (
    emp_id       NUMBER,
    old_salary   NUMBER,
    new_salary   NUMBER,
    changed_on   DATE
);

-- 3. Create the trigger
CREATE OR REPLACE TRIGGER trg_salary_audit
AFTER UPDATE OF salary ON employees_demo
FOR EACH ROW
BEGIN
    -- Insert record into audit table
    INSERT INTO salary_audit (emp_id, old_salary, new_salary, changed_on)
    VALUES (:OLD.emp_id, :OLD.salary, :NEW.salary, SYSDATE);
END;
/


How It Works
The trigger fires AFTER a salary is updated in employees_demo.
:OLD refers to the values before the update.
:NEW refers to the new values after the update.
The trigger inserts a log into salary_audit.



Test the Trigger
UPDATE employees_demo
SET salary = 65000
WHERE emp_id = 2;

SELECT * FROM salary_audit;

Sample Output
EMP_ID | OLD_SALARY | NEW_SALARY | CHANGED_ON
------------------------------------------------
2      | 60000      | 65000      | 11-AUG-25

#Web Application with Servlet (MVC)

#Servlet:(Controller)
	1. It is web component.
        2. defined as    RegisterServlet.java
        3. Servlet work as controller inside web application.
        4. it is used to handle client request and response.
        5. we don't write any business logic inside a servlet, we always write controller related logic.
        6. Servet always call Model to execute business logic.
        7. servlet-api.jar
        8. servlet.http.*;

Life Cycle: 
        a. init()
        b. service()
        c. destroy()

Servlet Execution Inside container:
        a. Client request came to Web container
        b. Web Container create two object RequestA and ResponseA
        c. RequestA object find the path of Servlet from web.xml
        d. when RequestA find the path of the servlet from web.xml then container start a thread for that client request.
        e. Thread will call  service()
        f. service() will call doGet() or doPost() based on the type of request
        g. output from doget()/doPost() is attached with the response object.
        h. container will use the response object and send it to client.

                             ServletEngine
        RegisterServlet.java =========>     RegisterServlet.class


Java Class(Model)
        1. model contain business logic.
	2. model contain two type of class. A. Service and B. DAO
        3. model is responsible to connect with Database.

html (View)
        1. The UI layer - client is interacting with.
        2. It is implemented with html.
        


Limitation : 
        1. we have to write huge amount of java code using Servlet.
        2. we can not seperate presentation logic and controller logic.




#Web Application with JSP (MVC)

#JSP = Java Server Pages
     1. defined as  Hello.jsp 
     2. we can seperate presentation logic and controller logic.
     3. we don't have to compile
     4. we have to write very less number of java code inside jsp page.


                                  JSP Engine                           ServeltEngine
           RegisterController.jsp  ========>   RegisterController.java  ==========>   RegisterController.class  



Life Cycle: 
        a. jsp-init()
        b. jsp-service()
        c. jsp-destroy()



Limitation :
       1. we don't have single request handler.so we can't implement security(authentication and authorization)
       2. we can not seperate controller logic from presentation logic




MVC Framework :(Single Request Handler Servelt)


	Struts 1.1.  => 2001       ActionServelt 
	JSF          => 2003       FacesServlet 
        ADF          => 2002       ADFController
        OAF          => 2003       OAFController
        Spring       => 2004       DispatcherServlet       





#Spring:

    //Spring Introduction 

                   1.   It is used to create   Enterprise Level Application
 
                          initial name -    Interface  21
                      2004     -     Spring 1.0  (Rod Jhonson)   Spring  Organization
                      2013     -     Spring  4.x  Pivotal
                      2018     -     Spring  5.0
                      2022     -     Spring  5.3		


                   2.  Spring  Framework is Light Weight Component     

                   3.  Spring Framework support loosely coupled architecture

		    4.  Different tier will interact with each other using POJO 

			POJO = Bean class =  Plain Old Java Object



		View         controller          service         DAO            DB

		

                    5.  Open Source
                    6.  Easy  to   Unit  Test
                    7.  Spring Support  Dependency Injection (Inversion Of Control)

                                                          



                        Tightly coupled                                                                                            

                                                  class      SearchFlightService{

                                                           SearchFlightDAO  obj=new SearchFlightDAO()
                                                  }

                                                   class   SearchFlightDAO{

                                                  }
                                            
                         Loosely Coupled
                                              
                                                 interface    C{

                                                  }

                                                 class      A  extends  C{

                                                           C       ref  = new   B();       
                                                  }

                                                   class   B  extends  C{

                                                  }

                     //Spring Framework  Setup : (with maven)

                                1.   Create  a maven Project.

					<dependency>
      						<groupId>org.springframework</groupId>

    					</dependency>

        "https://www.springframework.org/dtd/spring-beans-2.0.dtd">

    <bean id="obj"  class="org.example.Test"/>
</beans>

			                 4. Client.java

#Container Bean Scope:

            1. Singleton scope
 			A. default scope
			B. container always create only one object for multiple request.

	    2. Prototype scope
                        A. it is not default scope
			B. container  create multiple object for multiple request.




	singleton                             					prototype

	container always create only one object for multiple request.           container  create multiple object for multiple request.

        container create object when server stared.                             container create object when client request come.




                                                                    

                                   // Task Of   IOC  Container :
                                                   1.  Creation Of Object
                                                   2.  Initialize Object Attribute
                                                   3.  Manage the lifecycle of the object


                                    //  IOC Container : Core and J2EE 
                                                     
                                  
                                     J2EE   container  / Application Context Container
                                                                          s-1  create  java project
                                                                          s-2  include all the spring jars
                                                                          s-3  add  spring.xml
                                                                          s-4  add client.java and Emp.java
                                 
                                                       loading                        client request
                                  object creation         YES                               NO


                                     //  IOC Container : Scope

                                                                  1. singleton  - only one object will create for multiple request
                                                                                           - default scope of IOC Container

                                                                  2. prototype - multiple object will create for multiple request
                                                                                            - not default scope of IOC Container


                                                    //singleton

                                                                                                        Application Context
                                                    loading                                                       YES                     

                                                    client request                                                NO



                                                      //prototype

                                                                                    Bean factory                       Application Context
                                                    loading                         NO                                         NO                    

                                                    client request                  YES                                        YES

#DI for Primitive Type(setter)

		1. Create Maven Project.
		2. Add the dependency.
		3. Create spring.xml inside resources folder
                4. Create a POJO class.



//spring.xml
<!DOCTYPE beans PUBLIC "-//SPRING//DTD BEAN 2.0//EN"
        "https://www.springframework.org/dtd/spring-beans-2.0.dtd">
<beans>
    <bean id="obj1" class="org.example.Student">
        <property name="studId" value="1001"/>
        <property name="studName" value="Srini"/>
        <property name="studDesc" value="srini@gmail.com"/>
    </bean>
</beans>

//bean class//POJO
public class Student {
    private int studId;
    private String studName;
    private String studDesc;
   //getters,setters,constructor,toString()
}

//client.java
public class Client {
    public static void main(String args[]){

        ApplicationContext ac=new ClassPathXmlApplicationContext("classpath*:spring.xml");
        ac.getBean("obj1");
    }
}

//client.java
package org.example;

import org.springframework.context.ApplicationContext;
import org.springframework.context.support.ClassPathXmlApplicationContext;

public class Client {
    public static void main(String args[]){

        ApplicationContext ac=new ClassPathXmlApplicationContext("classpath*:spring.xml");
        System.out.println(ac.getBean("cobj1"));
    }
}


//student.java

package org.example;

public class Student {
    private int studId;
    private String studName;
    private String studDesc;

    public Student(int studId, String studName, String studDesc) {
        this.studId = studId;
        this.studName = studName;
        this.studDesc = studDesc;
        System.out.println("3 arg para const called");
    }


    public Student(String studName, String studDesc) {
        this.studName = studName;
        this.studDesc = studDesc;
        System.out.println("2 arg para both string const called");
    }

    public Student(int studId) {
        this.studId = studId;
        System.out.println("1 arg para const called");
    }

    public Student(int studId, String studName) {
        this.studId = studId;
        this.studName = studName;
        System.out.println("2 arg one int and other string para const called");
    }

    public Student() {
        System.out.println("default const called");
    }

    public int getStudId() {
        return studId;
    }

    public void setStudId(int studId) {
        this.studId = studId;
        System.out.println("studid setter called");
    }

    public void setStudName(String studName) {
        this.studName = studName;
        System.out.println("studName setter called");
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Student{" +
                "studId=" + studId +
                ", studName='" + studName + '\'' +
                ", studDesc='" + studDesc + '\'' +
                '}';
    }

    public void setStudDesc(String studDesc) {
        this.studDesc = studDesc;
    }

    public String getStudName() {
        return studName;
    }

    public String getStudDesc() {
        return studDesc;
    }
}


                   DI for Primitive Type
   			1.Setter Injection -  IOC container inject attribute inside POJO using setter
   			2.Constructor Injection -  IOC container inject attribute inside POJO using constructor


                                        //Setter Injection 
                                                        -  IOC container inject attribute inside POJO using setter

                                                                          s-1  create  java project
                                                                          s-2  include all the spring jars
                                                                          s-3  add  spring.xml
                                                                          s-4  add client.java and Dept.java (getters and setters)

                                        //Constructor  Injection
                                                       - IOC container inject attribute inside POJO using constructor
                                                       - if we have  two overloaded constructor then by default it will call 
                                                          constructor with string.
                                                       - by using type container will overload the constructor
                                                       - if we have more than one parameter then we can use index to 
                                                          point each position in the constructor.

                                                                          s-1  create  java project
                                                                          s-2  include all the spring jars
                                                                          s-3  add  spring.xml
                                                                          s-4  add client.java 
                                                                          s-5  Dept.java (constructor)


 

#DI for Secundary Type:
     - when container inject one class object inside another class.




          class   Car{

                
                Engine obj=new Engine();

	  }


	  class  Engine{



	  }

                                             
          s-1  create  maven java project
          s-2  include all the spring dependency
          s-3  add  spring.xml
          s-4  add client.java 
          s-5  Car.java , Engine.java


//spring.xml
<!DOCTYPE beans PUBLIC "-//SPRING//DTD BEAN 2.0//EN"
        "https://www.springframework.org/dtd/spring-beans-2.0.dtd">
<beans>
    <bean id="carObj" class="org.example.Car">
        <property name="carName" value="ford"/>
        <property name="modelNo" value="asdfbn2345" />
        <property name="engine"  ref="engObj" />
    </bean>

    <bean id="engObj" class="org.example.Engine">
        <property name="yearR" value="2025"/>
    </bean>
</beans>



//Car.java
public class Car {
    private String carName;
    private String modelNo;

    Engine engine;

    public Engine getEngine() {
        return engine;
    }

    public void setEngine(Engine engine) {
        this.engine = engine;
    }

    public Car() {
    }

    public String getCarName() {
        return carName;
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Car{" +
                "carName='" + carName + '\'' +
                ", modelNo='" + modelNo + '\'' +
                '}';
    }

    public void setCarName(String carName) {
        this.carName = carName;
        System.out.println("car name setter called");
    }

    public void setModelNo(String modelNo) {
        this.modelNo = modelNo;
    }

    public String getModelNo() {
        return modelNo;
    }

    public Car(String carName, String modelNo) {
        this.carName = carName;
        this.modelNo = modelNo;
    }
}



//Engine.java
package org.example;

public class Engine {
    private String yearR;

    public Engine(String yearR) {
        this.yearR = yearR;
    }

    public String getYearR() {
        return yearR;
    }

    public void setYearR(String yearR) {
        this.yearR = yearR;
        System.out.println("yearR setter called");
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return "Engine{" +
                "yearR='" + yearR + '\'' +
                '}';
    }

    public Engine() {
    }
}


//Client.java

package org.example;
import org.springframework.context.ApplicationContext;
import org.springframework.context.support.ClassPathXmlApplicationContext;

public class Client {
    public static void main(String args[]){


        ApplicationContext ac = new ClassPathXmlApplicationContext("classpath*:spring.xml");

        Car  c= (Car) ac.getBean("carObj");


        System.out.println(c);
        System.out.println(c.getEngine().getYearR());


    }
}
